# 9. Coolant Salt Chemistry and Tritium Control

The eutectic mixture formed from $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and NaF (92-8 mole %), melting point $383^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , is the proposed MSBR coolant salt. In projected pump-loop experiments (see this report, Sect. 5), engineering experience with this salt will be gained over a period of several years.

Although its cost and most of its chemical and physical properties are favorable for its adoption as the coolant, the fluoroborate salt suffers potential disadvantages, as compared with the $^7\mathrm{LiF - BeF}_2$ coolant used in the MSRE; the chief disadvantage is that the fluoroborate coolant exhibits a significant vapor pressure at operating temperatures and would exert an even higher vapor pressure if there was accidental mixing of the coolant and fuel salts.

The strong possibility that the coolant salt will have to be the sink for the tritium produced in an MSBR places an additional criterion on this or any other coolant to be used in an MSBR; the coolant should be able to contain a sufficient concentration of hydrogenous species to exchange isotopically with the tritium passing into the coolant circuit. At the same time the coolant with the hydrogenous additive should be compatible with its alloy containment system.

Success in meeting the performance criteria requires very carefully conducted research efforts. The investigations described below are motivated by the desire to meet these criteria. At the current stage of development, none of these efforts has advanced to the point where it provides a clear solution to the problems associated with the application of fluoroborates as coolants, although some results seem encouraging. (R.E.Thoma)

# 9.1 STUDIES OF HYDROGEN EVOLUTION AND TRITIUM EXCHANGE IN FLUOROBORATE COOLANT

S. Cantor R. M. Waller

We seek to determine how much chemically bound hydrogen can be retained in molten fluoroborate coolant. To the extent that this hydrogen does not corrode metals in contact with the coolant, it is available for isotopic exchange with any tritium entering the coolant circuit of an MSBR.

In these experiments evacuated nickel capsules containing $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ -NaF (92-8 mole $\%$ ) and metal coupons are heated within a silica vessel which is connected to gas-handling and pressure-measuring apparatus. Hydrogen (or tritium) gas diffusing through the nickel capsule can be readily determined since vessels of fused silica are virtually impermeable to hydrogen.

In a previously reported $^{1}$ experiment, hydrogen (in concentrations equivalent to 60 ppm $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ ) in the salt reacted with chromium coupons at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and was completely converted to $\mathrm{H}_2(\mathrm{g})$ . The evolved hydrogen was initially established by gas-chromatographic analyses; the loss of hydrogen from the salt sample was confirmed by a subsequent tritium tracer analysis.

Two experimental runs (see Table 9.1) performed with nickel as the only metal in contact with the salt indicated that low, but encouraging, levels of chemically bound hydrogen can be retained in the salt. In both runs the salt contained hydrogenous impurities incorporated during the recrystallization of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ from aqueous media. Although the postexperimental examination of the salt after both runs confirmed isotopic exchange, it should be noted that the salt samples could not be stirred in these experiments and perhaps had not achieved equilibrium. Further tests will be necessary to verify isotopic exchange. Should isotopic exchange be confirmed, experiments in a circulating salt system will be required to establish isotopic exchange in the coolant as a practical method for tritium control.

Five experiments were carried out with nickel coupons in which NaOH and $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ were encapsulated with the coolant salt. The amounts of hydrogen added with the NaOH and $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ were many times greater than the hydrogen believed to be bound in the fluoroborate itself. Two capsules containing 0.4 mole $\%$ NaOH were maintained at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , one for 192 hr and the other for 387 hr. In both cases the evolved hydrogen gas, determined chromatographically, was equivalent to the chemically bound hydrogen loaded into the capsules.

Table 9.1. Details of two experiments involving isotopic exchange in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF   

<table><tr><td>Run No.</td><td>Hydrogenous impurity at beginning (μeq H/g salt)</td><td>Conditions and procedure</td><td>H2(g) accounting (μeq H/g salt)</td><td>Analytical examination of salt sample after opening capsule</td></tr><tr><td>6, nickel capsule and coupons</td><td>6.7 ± 0.7a</td><td>80 hr at 520°C; cool down over weekend; 10 hr at 600°C; during and between heating, gas samples were collected and analyzed chromatographically. Capsule and silica vessel then evacuated for 22 hr at 600°C; T2(g), 0.1 Ci, introduced into system, which was maintained at 600°C for 140 hr.</td><td>5.0 ± 0.5, all collected prior to the 22-hr evacuation.</td><td>By counting tritium in the salt and by prior mass-spectrographic analysis of H2(g) and HT(g); it was deduced that 0.5 ± 0.1 μeq of H per gram of salt had been retained in the salt.</td></tr><tr><td>16, nickel capsule and coupons</td><td>14 ± 1.4a</td><td>102 hr at 600°C; during most of this time the silica vessel was evacuated by means of a Toepler pump from which exiting gas was analyzed for H2(g); then D2(g) at about 22 torrs was introduced and maintained at 600°C for 90 hr.</td><td>14 ± 1.4, collected prior to introduction of D2(g).</td><td>Infrared absorption spectrum of 15-mg pellet of salt showed an -OD peak whose absorbance was 31/2 times greater than the -OH peak; however, neither peak provides a quantitative analysis for hydrogen in the salt.</td></tr></table>

${}^{a}$ Assuming a ${10}\%$ relative standard error in the gas-chromatographic analysis.

Two capsules containing 0.5 mole % boric acid were heated at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , one for 435 hr and the other for 644 hr. In these time intervals the evolved hydrogen gas, when analyzed, corresponded to about $60\%$ of the hydrogen introduced into the capsule. However, in a third capsule, with an initial concentration of 0.66 mole $\% \mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ and heated at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for 300 hr, virtually all the chemically bound hydrogen in the capsule escaped as $\mathrm{H}_{2}(\mathrm{g})$ . The disparity between the first two $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ experiments and the third can probably be attributed to inadvertent pumping away of $\mathrm{H}_{2}$ in the first two experiments.

Post experimental infrared spectral examination (by John Bates) of the salts in these five latter experiments revealed low OH concentrations, hardly different from that found in fluoroborates, without added NaOH or $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ , that had been subjected to similar treatment. These infrared results, together with the quantities of $\mathrm{H}_{2}(\mathrm{g})$ collected during the experimental runs, suggest that the NaOH and $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ did not augment the capacity of the coolant salt to retain hydrogen. Furthermore, the higher level of protons increased the corrosion of nickel. After the experiments the salt and capsule walls were examined and found to contain an easily observable yellowish skin, identified as $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ . With the fluoroborate salt alone, such corrosion products, though possibly formed, have not been detected.

# 9.2 REACTION OF SODIUM FLUORIDE-SODIUM TETRAFLUOROBORATE WITH WATER

H.W.Kohn

Attempts to refine our previous measurements of the equilibrium constants for the reactions

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {H F} \tag {1}
$$

and

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} \rightarrow \mathrm {N a B F} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {H F} \tag {2}
$$

by improving analytical techniques for the transpiration method have so far met with failure. These have included installation of thermal conductivity apparatus to measure gas composition, sodium fluoride traps for absorption of HF and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ from the gas stream, and azeotropic distillation from pyridine solutions of samples of the effluent gas collected from the transpiration experiment. During the course of these experiments, we have observed some HF present at all times (nickel containers and copper lines and samplers are used). Consequently, our approach has been to determine

more accurately the role of HF in stabilizing $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$ and in producing $\mathrm{H}_{2}\mathrm{O}$ .

We have improved on some earlier experimental results by using more refined analytical techniques to measure the extent of reactions (1) and (2). By collecting the off-gas containing $\mathrm{BF}_3$ and HF in aqueous sodium fluoride followed by $\mathrm{CaCl}_2$ precipitation:

$$
\mathrm {B F} _ {3} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {a q}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N a B F} _ {4}, \tag {3}
$$

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} + 2 \mathrm {C a C l} _ {2} + 3 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \rightarrow \mathrm {H} _ {3} \mathrm {B O} _ {3}
$$

$$
+ \underline {{2 C a F _ {2} \downarrow}} + N a C l + 3 H C l, \tag {4}
$$

$$
2 \mathrm {H F} + \mathrm {C a C l} _ {2} \rightarrow \mathrm {C a F} _ {2} \downarrow + 2 \mathrm {H C l}, \tag {5}
$$

followed by a double-end-point titration of the resultant HCl and $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ -mannitol complex, we ascertained that in fluoroborate to which $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ was added slowly as vapor over a period of several hours, there was always a small pressure of HF which would persist for two or three days. The HF pressure measurements so obtained are still erratic since, for example, any error in $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ determination is reflected threefold in the determination of HF, but the implication is that under our experimental conditions (nickel containers, copper lines, $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) the equilibrium content of HF in the argon sweep gas is approximately 0.1 meq/liter. Part of the difficulty stems from the apparent high solubility of HF in fluoroborate at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

We believe that the HF is due to reaction (2) above. Using the following numbers, oxide $= 400$ ppm obtained by inventory of the $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , and HF $= 0.1$ meq/liter, we can calculate an equilibrium quotient $= [\mathrm{HF}][\mathrm{O}^{2-}] / [\mathrm{OH}^{-}] = 10^{-4}$ at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ (when HF is expressed in atmospheres and the other quantities are expressed in moles per liter). The OH is calculated from the vapor pressure of water and the flow rate of argon through a side stream. We assume all the water reacts, since virtually none is found, under these conditions, by Karl Fischer titration of the effluent gas. Using the temperature variation of the equilibrium determined previously, this means that (at the same oxide and hydroxide levels) the equilibrium pressure of HF required to stabilize hydroxide at $650^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ would be about $50\mathrm{mm}$ .

We have obtained preliminary values for the solubility of HF in the NaF-NaBF $_4$ eutectic mixture with HF at

![](images/5245c7f0f9eeb002d118300a42127856c5648adef9d21760b58f4b437274d6c4.jpg)  
Fig. 9.1. Henry's law solubility of HF in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4 - 9\%$ NaF eutectic.

0.29 atm in a nickel vessel. The HF was supplied in a gas mixture composed of Ar, $\mathrm{BF}_3$ , $\mathrm{H}_2$ , and HF. Solubility was determined by sparging the HF from the saturated melt; the amounts of HF removed were measured by alkalimetric titration. The removal of HF was first order as expected, but the curve of concentration in the gas vs sparging time showed a long tail; that is, the last traces of HF were difficult to remove. The value of the Henry's law constant at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is $0.33 \pm 0.03$ equiv liter $^{-1}$ atm $^{-1}$ . During the three measurements the oxide concentration ranged from 500 to 300 ppm, determined by $\mathrm{KBrF}_4$ , but the change in oxide content did not seem to affect the solubility markedly in this range.

Solubilities at other temperatures were measured in three different ways: (1) by saturation and stripping, (2) by saturation at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ followed by raising the temperature and titrating the HF in the cover gas, and (3) by saturation at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , followed by raising the temperature and measuring the corresponding pressure rise and subsequently correcting it for expansion of the cover gas and for the contribution from $\mathrm{BF}_3$ overpressure. In Fig. 9.1 the results of these three methods are presented, along with a correlation developed by

Shaffer and Watson<sup>3</sup> which related HF solubility in NaF-BeF<sub>2</sub> and in NaF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> with the free fluoride content of the melt. The Shaffer-Watson formula is given below.

$$
K = \exp \left(\frac {A + B R C}{R T} - \frac {D + E R C}{R}\right)
$$

where

$A, B, D,$ and $E$ are empirical constants,

$C$ is the fraction of free fluoride in the melt,

$R$ is the gas constant,

$T$ is the absolute temperature,

$K$ is the Henry's law constant.

One may extend this correlation to the $\mathrm{NaF - BF_3}$ system by using the vapor pressure of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ over the eutectic from Cantor's data4 to calculate the free

fluoride in the mixture as a function of temperature and use these numbers in the Shaffer-Watson formula to calculate HF solubility. The results of this calculation are shown on the graph.

A more refined apparatus for measuring HF solubility has been constructed and is presently being tested.

We have also used infrared spectroscopy to determine the stability constant referred to before. This is reported separately.

# 9.3 IDENTIFICATION OF CORROSION PRODUCTS IN THE BUBBLER TUBE OF THE FLUOROBORATE TEST LOOP

S. Cantor

For approximately 1.3 years, an Inconel pump loop (operated by the Reactor Division under the supervision of A. N. Smith) circulated molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 mole%). Welded into the pump bowl was a $1/2$ -in.-ID Inconel tube through which helium and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ entered the circulating salt. After shutdown of the loop, the lower part of this tube was cut off, sectioned, and split axially. One half of the axially split portion was submitted to the Reactor Chemistry Division for chemical examination of the corrosion and mass transfer products deposited on the inner surfaces of the tube.

A full description of the appearance of the bubbler tube is given elsewhere in this report. Five corrosion and mass transfer products were identified by x-ray diffraction and electron microprobe analyses:

1. a green salt, $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$   
2. a yellow salt, $\mathrm{NaNiF_3}$   
3. a black powdery magnetic material with the stoichiometry $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Fe}$ ;   
4. a porous, gray metallic mass, which plugged the mouth of the tube, composed of nickel metal and $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ in approximately equal parts;   
5. a millimeter-thick layer, running through most of the length of the tube, composed of nickel and nickel oxide.

A plausible inference which may account in part for the morphology of the deposit assumes that $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ , $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ , and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Fe}$ were present in a surface scum on the fluoroborate melt prior to migrating up the bubbler

tube; the migration possibly occurred when liquid droplets were formed by bubbles breaking at the mouth of the bubbler. A second transfer mechanism might have been backflow up the tube; however, such events were infrequent and of short duration.

The presence of the salts $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ and $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ has been noted previously as corrosion products from molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . Both are only slightly soluble in the melt. They arise initially through oxidation of the metal. In the case of $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ , the oxidation of chromium in the loop probably arose from three sources:

1. $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ entered as an impurity in the helium and possibly in the fluoroborate salt charge (and also on one occasion deliberately introduced); the reactions may be written:

$$
\mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} (\mathrm {g}) + 2 \mathrm {F} ^ {-} (\text {i n t h e s a l t}) \rightarrow 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} (\text {i n t h e s a l t}),
$$

$$
\mathrm {C r} + 3 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + 3 \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {l}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N a} _ {3} \mathrm {C r F} _ {6} + \frac {3}{2} \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}).
$$

2. Air trapped in lines when tanks of gas were changed; besides the moisture thereby introduced, $\mathrm{O}_2(\mathrm{g})$ would also lead to oxidation:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {C r} + 3 / _ {4} \mathrm {O} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + 3 \mathrm {N a F (I)} \\ + \mathrm {B F} _ {3} (\mathrm {g}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N a} _ {3} \mathrm {C r F} _ {6} + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {B} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {3} (\mathrm {l}). \\ \end{array}
$$

3. Metallic ions already present in the melt oxidize chromium; for instance, the fluoroborate charged into the loop contained about 200 ppm iron; the reaction with chromium may be written:

$$
\mathrm {C r} + ^ {3 / 2} \mathrm {F e} ^ {2 +} (\mathrm {l}) \rightarrow \mathrm {C r} ^ {3 +} (\mathrm {l}) + ^ {3 / 2} \mathrm {F e}.
$$

The trivalent chromium subsequently precipitated as $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$

In accounting for the formation of $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ , reactions similar to those given under 1 and 2 above seem likely:

$$
\mathrm {N i} + 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {l}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} + \mathrm {H} _ {2},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {N i} + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {O} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N a F (l)} \\ + \frac {2}{3} \mathrm {B F} _ {3} (\mathrm {g}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} + \frac {1}{3} \mathrm {B} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {3} (\mathrm {l}). \\ \end{array}
$$

The reasons for the formation of $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Fe}$ are not easily ascertained. One can speculate that ionic iron and nickel in the salt oxidize metallic chromium and

coprecipitate as an alloy:

$$
\frac {8 \%}{3} \mathrm {C r} + 3 \mathrm {N i} ^ {2 +} (1) + \mathrm {F e} ^ {2 +} (1) \rightarrow \frac {8 \%}{3} \mathrm {C r} ^ {3 +} (1) + \left| \mathrm {N i} _ {3} \mathrm {F e} \right..
$$

Most of the $\mathrm{Cr}^{3+}$ would subsequently precipitate as $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ . The dissolved ionic nickel and iron could have originated by means of the oxidation by $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O(g)}$ and $\mathrm{O}_2(\mathrm{g})$ .

The origin of the "extra" nickel in the tube (noted above under products 4 and 5) is uncertain. The nickel plug formed in a position where the salt was exposed to lower temperatures; perhaps the reaction

$$
\mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\text {d i s s o l v e d i n m e l t}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) \rightleftharpoons 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N i}
$$

permitted the deposition of nickel at the mouth of the tube.

In summary, the corrosion products identified in the bubbler tube originated with the introduction of oxidants, the most important being water vapor. The water vapor led to the formation of HF, which corroded the Inconel. This corrosion, which implies the dissolution of chromium, iron, and nickel ions, eventually led to the precipitation of $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ and $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ , some of which were carried on the surface of the molten fluoroborate. Dissolved iron and nickel also reacted further with metallic chromium and were apparently precipitated out as the alloy $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Fe}$ . Some of this alloy was also present on the surface of the molten salt.

# 9.4 MASS SPECTROSCOPY OF FLUOROBORATE MSR COOLANTS

C.F.Weaver J.D.Redman

With respect to tritium control in a molten-salt reactor, F. F. Blankenship has recently suggested that hydrogenous material might be added to and maintained in the proposed coolant salt, $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 mole $\%$ ), by incorporating 1 or $2\%$ NaOH into the liquid if the behavior of this system as such or its corrosive attack on structural materials is acceptable. Consequently, we have initiated studies of the vapors over such materials using a time-of-flight mass spectrometer to detect the species effusing from a Knudsen cell.

The first experiments were made with the separate components $\mathrm{BF}_3$ , $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , and $\mathrm{NaOH}$ in nickel cells to determine cracking patterns and to confirm that the boron compounds behaved as commonly described.

The $\mathrm{BF}_3$ yielded a cracking pattern essentially like that in the literature, and no polymers of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ were observed. The $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ decomposed, yielding $\mathrm{BF}_3$ monomer as the only vapor species. However, the NaOH

behaved in a more complex way. In addition to the previously reported monomer and dimer, both the trimer and tetramer of $\mathrm{NaOH}$ were observed. Water was also evolved from material which had been dried and outgassed at $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . This was attributed to the decomposition $2\mathrm{NaOH} \rightarrow \mathrm{Na}_{2}\mathrm{O} + \mathrm{H}_{2}\mathrm{O}\uparrow$ . In addition a small amount of $\mathrm{H}_{2}$ was evolved, probably by $\mathrm{Ni} + 2\mathrm{NaOH} \rightarrow \mathrm{Na}_{2}\mathrm{O} + \mathrm{NiO} + \mathrm{H}_{2}$ , although the condensed products of this reaction are not well established. By far the most intense of the reduced species was sodium vapor, attributed to the reaction $\mathrm{Na}_{2}\mathrm{O} + \mathrm{Ni} \rightarrow \mathrm{NiO}\downarrow + 2\mathrm{Na}\uparrow$ . The Na vapor was detected by mass spectrometry and the NiO precipitate by x-ray diffraction. Severe attack of the nickel cell was also evident by direct observation. The standard free energy change for the reaction as written at $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ is $+29\mathrm{kcal}$ , which implies that $P_{\mathrm{Na}} = 7 \times 10^{-2}$ torr, well within the range of detection of the mass spectrometer.

The possibility of adding hydrogenous material to the fluoroborate coolant as water either intentionally or accidentally as a steam leak suggested that the reaction of moisture with $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ should also be investigated.

Two approaches were used to obtain reaction products of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4\mathrm{-H}_2\mathrm{O}$ system. One was to add $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ $(0.1\mathrm{g})$ to $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ $(0.2\mathrm{g})$ and to reflux at $300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ under an atmosphere of helium for an hour. After refluxing, the cell was cooled, evacuated, and studied in the normal manner. Vapor over the temperature range of 25 to $750^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was quite similar to that over pure $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , even to the amount of water vapor evolved. No reaction products had been formed, at least to the $300^{\circ}$ refluxing temperature limit; moreover, the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ displayed a very impressive, nondeliquescent character.

In the second approach to obtain reaction products from the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ - $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ system, steam was admitted over the temperature range 25 to $750^{\circ}$ at an approximate leak rate of $10^{-7}$ torr liter $\mathrm{sec}^{-1}$ . The complexity of the reaction was apparent. For convenience, an attempt was made to assign fragments to possible molecular precursors, almost arbitrarily (Table 9.2). The only

Table 9.2. Partial pressure of assumed molecules in vapor at $300^{\circ}$ from the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4\cdot \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ reaction   

<table><tr><td>Molecules</td><td>Pressure (torrs)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>× 10-2</td></tr><tr><td>H2O</td><td>1.0</td></tr><tr><td>BF3</td><td>2.5</td></tr><tr><td>HF</td><td>1.0</td></tr><tr><td>NaBOF2</td><td>0.008</td></tr><tr><td>BF3·H2O</td><td>0.2</td></tr><tr><td>NaBF2</td><td>0.003</td></tr><tr><td>? (mass 19)</td><td>0.01</td></tr></table>

criterion was to group fragments displaying the same coefficient of temperature dependence with the same precursor. Some fragments certainly had more than one precursor. Nevertheless, at $300^{\circ}$ , reaction products of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and steam accounted for at least $30\%$ of the total vapor pressure, most of which was HF.

# 9.5 SPECTROSCOPIC INVESTIGATIONS OF HYDROGEN- AND DEUTERIUM-CONTAINING IMPURITIES IN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ AND NaF-NaBF $_4$ EUTECTICS

John B. Bates

Harold W. Kohn

Jack P. Young

Marvin M. Murray

George E. Boyd

Evidence for the existence of hydrogen-containing impurities in $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ was first obtained from near-infrared spectra of the molten salt and in mid-infrared spectra of pressed pellets of the "pure" crystalline material. Bands observed in the region between 1.35 and $2.55\mu$ (Fig.9.2) are believed to correspond to overtones and combinations of fundamental vibrations of an OH species (either free $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$ or $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ ) in the melt. Although the vibrational spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ has been thoroughly investigated,[7-9] no infrared measurements have been reported in the region above $3000~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ with thick samples for transmission measurements.

We have recently repeated infrared transmission measurements on a single crystal of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ supplied by L. O. Gilpatrick. This crystal was polished to a thickness of about $1/4$ to $1/2$ mm before examination with a Perkin-Elmer model 621 spectrophotometer. A single sharp band at $3641\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ was observed which exhibited about a 2:1 dichroic ratio as the infrared polarizer was rotated through an angle of $90^{\circ}$ . This band was assigned tentatively to an OH-containing molecular species (most likely $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ ) which is effectively isolated in an $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ matrix. Subsequent spectroscopic studies[10] on relatively pure samples of crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ appear to confirm this assignment.

Spectroscopic observation of isotopic exchange of hydrogen with deuterium was investigated in solid-state and in molten-salt exchange reactions. In the first experiments, $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ vapor was bubbled slowly into a

ORNL-DWG.71-3460

![](images/9e2c32e349b38085de71a8b1601e845c0570b79053c55d3de2934fd99a2a0a26.jpg)  
Fig. 9.2. Transmittance and absorbance of a $0.15\mathrm{-mg}$ pellet of a $\mathbf{D}_2$ -treated sample of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$

molten $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ eutectic at $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The amount of deuterium introduced into the melt corresponded to about 200 ppm $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ . Two sharp bands were observed at 3641 and $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ , respectively, in the infrared spectra of pellets pressed from a quenched sample of this melt. The band at $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ corresponds to the OD stretching frequency of an impurity assumed to be $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OD}$ . A quantitative determination of the amount of OH impurity in the starting $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ eutectic and of the percent of isotopic exchange could not be made; but, judging from relative peak intensities of the 3641- and $2688\text{-cm}^{-1}$ bands, the OD concentration was estimated to be about $30\%$ of the OH concentration. Much larger amounts of the OD-containing impurity were incorporated when $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ vapor was passed over solid $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ at $377^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The OD concentration in these samples was estimated to be 3600 ppm by titrating DF liberated in the reaction

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} + \mathrm {D} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \rightarrow \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O D} + \mathrm {D F}.
$$

The extent of reaction and exchange of DF with $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ was studied by equilibrating molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ at $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with DF and argon. Subsequent infrared measurements on pressed pellets of the solid material obtained from quenching this melt also revealed two sharp bands at 3641 and $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . The infrared spectrum of a $\sim 0.25$ -mm-thick pellet of the DF-treated $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ in the region above $2000~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ is shown in Fig. 9.3. This spectrum is typical of others obtained with

![](images/3ccdb2579477cf6bc0144feaad68f82055baf88eafe54bb559e3fa64df2868b7.jpg)  
Fig. 9.3. Infrared spectrum of a $100\mathrm{-mg}$ pellet of DF-treated $\mathbf{NaBF}_4$

samples of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ which had been treated with $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ . The production of the OD-containing material in this case could occur by reaction of DF with oxides (e.g., $\mathrm{DF} + \mathrm{BF}_2\mathrm{O}^- \rightarrow \mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ) and by isotopic exchange with the OH-containing impurity.

Solid samples of KF, KBr, and $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ were treated with DF at $350^{\circ}C$ to check on the possibility that the bands at 3641 and $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ may be caused by HF and DF, respectively, dissolved in $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ . Alkalimetry showed that the solids after treatment contained 5.46, 0.25, and 0.097 meq/g of acid respectively. No band at $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ was detectable, although a weak band at $3641~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ appeared in the spectrum of the $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ sample. The results from this experiment appeared to confirm our earlier contention that the bands at 3641 and $2688~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ were not caused by dissolved HF and DF respectively.

The results of the above experiments demonstrated that $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ contained an "OH impurity" (probably $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ ) and that the deuterium-substituted form of this material could be produced in molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ by reaction with the $\mathrm{BF_4}^-$ ( $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ ) or with oxide impurities (DF) and possibly by isotopic exchange. The extent of isotopic exchange alone was investigated with a sample of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ prepared by S. Cantor which was sealed in a nickel capsule and heated for about three days at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in an atmosphere of $\mathrm{D}_2$ gas ( $\sim 25~\mathrm{mm}$ ). The infrared spectrum of a pressed pellet of this sample is shown in Fig. 9.4. The ratio of the absorbance of OD to that of OH is about 3:1. The results of this experiment appear to indicate an exchange of atomic deuterium for the H in OH, although it is also possible to interpret these results in terms of the reaction

$$
\mathrm {D} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N i} + 2 \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} (\text {i n t h e m e l t}) \rightleftharpoons
$$

$$
2 \mathrm {O D} ^ {-} (\text {i n t h e m e l t}) + \mathrm {N i}.
$$

ORNL-DWG. 71-3461

![](images/928955741b8c9aa886c7b735345acc195de7a964baee1ac7870212f1246b91ee.jpg)  
Fig. 9.4. Band due to absorption by OD-containing species in the infrared spectrum of molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ at $425^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

The above experimental results demonstrated that samples of presumed high-purity $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ contained OH impurities and that exchange with deuterium atoms occurs in the molten state. We thus desired to obtain a direct measurement of the OH absorption in molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ as a function of time. It was observed that the bands due to overtones of OH disappear from the near-infrared spectrum of the melt in a matter of hours. However, pellets pressed from the frozen salt always showed the sharp absorption at $3641~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . These two observations can be reconciled if it is assumed that a small, but unknown, concentration of OH or $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ is in equilibrium with $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ . Indeed, the rate of decrease of the near-infrared OH peak does not appear to follow simple first-order kinetics. Therefore, competing, consecutive, or higher-order processes must be involved. It is interesting also to note that the same near-infrared spectrum is observed if either NaOH or $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ is added to molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ .

Near-infrared spectra of molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ were measured with $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ cells and a high-temperature furnace described previously. For measurements in the mid

infrared region, samples of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ were spiked with $\mathrm{D}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ because it was possible to study only the absorption by OD rather than that by OH with the available equipment. The infrared spectrum of a molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4 - D_3BO_3}$ solution in the $2700\text{-cm}^{-1}$ region at about $425^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is shown in Fig. 9.4. The broad OD band is centered at about $2690\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ , in excellent agreement with the frequency of the sharp band observed at 2688 $\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ in the pellet spectra (Fig. 9.3).

A slight decrease in the absorption of the $2690\mathrm{-cm}^{-1}$ band with time was observed. However, the band was still observable after 48 hr in a 2-mm-path-length $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ cell. Under our experimental conditions, the OD absorption in the melt was stable in the presence of added INOR-8 but unstable in the presence of pure chromium metal. It is not known how much OH or OD was actually observed in either the melt or pellet spectra. Since it was demonstrated that OD species can be seen spectrally in the melt, an apparatus which does not use $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ as a window material is being designed and fabricated for use in further studies on molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ .

The results of these spectroscopic studies indicate that an OH-containing impurity occurs in "pure" samples of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , that exchange with deuterium atoms takes place in the molten state, and that the OH species persists at melt temperatures of $425^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ over a period of days. Additional experiments are under way to determine quantitatively the amount of OH-containing impurity in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and the extent of isotopic exchange of H for D or T in the molten state at temperatures of about $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

# 9.6 RAMAN SPECTRA OF THE HIGH-TEMPERATURE PHASE OF POLYCRYSTALLINE $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$

Arvin S. Quist John B. Bates  
George E. Boyd

Raman spectra were measured with polycrystalline $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ at temperatures just above and below the dimorphic crystal transition $(245^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . The results of this study rationalize anomalies in x-ray diffraction data for $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and related structures. The spectrum of the high-temperature solid phase indicates the presence of a highly symmetric effective field about the $\mathrm{BF_4}^-$ ion similar to that previously observed in melt spectra. A complete disordering of the fluoride ion positions must be assumed for the spectral results to be consistent with recent x-ray data on crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ powders at $265^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which have been indexed in the hexagonal system.

The room-temperature crystalline form of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ is orthorhombic, space group Cmcm, and is isostructural only with the room-temperature forms of $\mathrm{CaSO_4}$ and $\mathrm{NaClO_4}$ . All three compounds undergo phase transitions at elevated temperatures. Sodium perchlorate has a cubic structure above $308^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ which is said to be isostructural with the high-temperature phases of many perchlorates, tetrafluoroborates, and sulfates. The high-temperature phase of $\mathrm{CaSO_4}$ (above $1210^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) was reported to be hexagonal. At $245\pm 1^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ undergoes a change in crystal structure to a form first reported as monoclinic. Dworkin and Bredig, however, have expressed the view that a lowering of the symmetry of the high-temperature form without a lowering of the number of molecules per unit cell is quite unlikely. They have indexed the powder pattern reported by Pistorius, Boeyens, and Clark on the basis that a mixture of the orthorhombic low-temperature phase with a high-temperature hexagonal phase was measured. Recently, Bredig obtained x-ray powder patterns of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ at $265^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with a Buerger precession camera. The powder photographs were indexed in the hexagonal system with parameters $a_0 = 5.00\pm 0.02$ and $c_{0} = 7.75\pm 0.03\AA$ . Acceptable agreement between observed and calculated diffraction intensities, however, could not be obtained for ordered hexagonal space groups.

In view of the uncertainties in the structure of the high-temperature phase of crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , we have measured the Raman spectrum of this material in an effort to provide additional information regarding the lattice symmetry. Static and dynamic field effects on the vibrational modes of $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ in the orthorhombic (room temperature) $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ structure have been established by previous studies. $^{15,16}$ The two $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ions in the primitive unit cell of this crystal occupy $C_{2\nu}$ sites, so that all the components of the doubly degenerate $\nu_{2}(e)$ mode and of the triply degenerate $\nu_{3}(f_{2})$ and $\nu_{4}(f_{2})$ modes are observed in the Raman spectrum. An increase in the site symmetry of the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion in the high-temperature form may cause a collapse of the

splitting, and hence only one frequency for each of the vibrational modes would be observed. The presence or absence of such effects in the spectra of the high-temperature phase would establish the symmetry of the effective field about the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion in the high-temperature lattice and perhaps also resolve the existing conflict in the x-ray data.

In the current Raman studies of the high-temperature phase of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ only the $\nu_{1}(a_{1}),\nu_{2}(e)$ ,and $\nu_{4}(f_{2})$ modes were examined in detail;17 the intensities of the $\nu_{3}(f_{2})$ modes are quite weak under these conditions. A detailed comparison of the $\nu_{2}$ and $\nu_{4}$ bands at 240 and $254^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is given in Fig. 9.5, which clearly shows the abrupt change in the Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ as it undergoes the phase transition.

The experimental observations indicate that the effective environment about the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion in the high-temperature crystalline phase of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ has tetrahedral (or higher) symmetry and that there is no measurable (or allowed) dynamic coupling between neighboring $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ions. This observation is consistent with the recent x-ray results, $^{14}$ which led to a hexagonal structure only if a complete disordering of the fluoride positions is assumed. With this complete disordering, the effective field experienced by a $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion would be highly symmetric, and the lack of splitting of the $\nu_{2}$ and $\nu_{4}$ bands in the Raman spectra could be explained. This structure also would be consistent with recent infrared spectra obtained to $270^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in which the $\nu_{1}$ mode disappeared in the high-temperature phase and the three components of $\nu_{4}$ observed at room temperature were reported to merge into a single component above the transition point. $^{18}$ Furthermore, the assumption of a disordered lattice structure explains the similarity between the Raman spectrum of the high-temperature crystalline phase and that of the melt. $^{19}$ In the high-temperature crystalline phase the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ions are undergoing large-amplitude random reorientations (librations), which gives rise to the disorder, and a given $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion "sees" an average symmetric field similar to that experienced in the melt. Because the neighboring $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ions are closer together in the high-temperature solid than they are in the melt, $^{20}$ the F-F nonbonded repulsion is larger in the solid and a higher $\nu_{1}$ frequency is observed.

![](images/63e3f9e36d1b037049942d4396dd18374dbda9013d30e31a2f5ce6b261895676.jpg)  
Fig. 9.5. Raman spectra of the $\nu_{2}$ and $\nu_{4}$ regions of polycrystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ at 240 and $254^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . A phase transition occurs at $245^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

A disordered hexagonal structure also can explain the observation of optical isotropy of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ in the high-temperature phase.21 The fluorine atoms make the largest contribution to the polarizability of the crystal, and their orientation will determine the symmetry of the crystal indicatrix. Complete disordering of the F atoms would give a nearly spherical indicatrix, and the crystal would appear to be optically isotropic.

# 9.7 A NEW METHOD FOR SYNTHESIS OF $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$

L. O. Gilpatrick C. J. Barton

Laboratory studies are continuing in attempts to synthesize hydrogen-containing species for the retention of tritium in the MSR coolant salt. One of these species is the first hydrolysis product of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ in aqueous solution, which has been reported to be $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ . [22,23] Ryss and Slutskaya [24] have reported a successful synthesis of this compound in which boric acid is reacted at $0^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with a saturated solution of $\mathrm{NaHF}_2$ as follows:

$$
2 \mathrm {N a H F} _ {2} + \mathrm {H} _ {3} \mathrm {B O} _ {3} \xrightarrow [ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} ]{0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {N a F}.
$$

This synthesis has the disadvantage that it produces a mixture of $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ and NaF and in addition also becomes contaminated with some $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ produced by a side reaction when attempts are made to recrystallize it from water solutions.

A new synthesis has been devised for $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ which employs the following reactions in a saturated solution of $\mathrm{NaHCO}_3$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {B F} _ {3} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \xrightarrow [ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} ]{\sim 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} \mathrm {H} (\mathrm {B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H}), \\ \mathrm {H} \left(\mathrm {B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H}\right) + \mathrm {N a H C O} _ {3} \xrightarrow [ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} ]{0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {C O} _ {2} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}. \\ \end{array}
$$

One mole of solid $\mathrm{NaHCO}_3$ and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ gas are added progressively to 5 moles of water at $0^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which produces a clear solution. Four volumes of cold $95\%$ ethanol are then added. Storage at $0^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ gives a good yield of product which has a better purity than that produced by the method of Ryss.

The identification of this material is relatively certain and direct based on its properties. Sodium hydroxytrifluoroborate is very water soluble and shows rapid hydrolysis to NaF, $\mathrm{H}_3\mathrm{BO}_3$ ,and $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ when stored as an aqueous solution. It is birefringent to polarized light and exhibits refractive indexes from a maximum of 1.350 to a minimum of about 1.343 at $25^{\circ}C.$ The crystals belong to the hexagonal system and are polar,

as has been shown by a recent x-ray structure study $^{25}$ which makes positive identification convenient.

# 9.8 SOLUBILITY OF $\mathbf{BF}_3$ GAS IN FLUORIDE MELTS

S. Cantor W. T. Ward

The purposes of these measurements are to relate $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubility to changes in the thermodynamic properties of molten fluorides and to provide data relevant to the use of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ as a burnable neutron poison for purposes of reactor control. The single-vessel apparatus and the procedures of measurement have been described previously.[26]

Solubilities of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ have been determined in five molten-salt solvents composed of LiF and $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ . The chief results obtained thus far are:

1. In each solvent, the higher the temperature, the lower the $\mathbf{BF}_3$ solubility; that is, the enthalpy of solution is exothermic.   
2. At constant temperature and pressure, the higher the concentration of LiF, the higher the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ ; indeed, the Henry's law constant appears to be linear with the thermodynamic activity of LiF.   
3. Henry's law is obeyed in almost all cases; however, where the concentration of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in the melt approaches 1 mole % or greater, there are discernible positive deviations from Henry's law.

The data and derived thermodynamic information are summarized in Table 9.3. The magnitude and negative sign of the enthalpy of solution suggest a strong interaction of $\mathrm{BF}_3(\mathrm{g})$ with the melt. There does not appear to be a correlation of enthalpy of solution (of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ ) with melt composition. The approximate constancy of $\Delta H$ in Table 9.3 signifies that plots of $\log K$ (Henry's law constant) vs reciprocal temperature (in $^\circ \mathrm{K}$ ) are approximately parallel.

As may be noted in column 3 in the Table, the higher the mole fraction of LiF, the higher the Henry's law constant. In Fig. 9.6, the Henry's law constant at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is plotted vs activity of LiF reported for these melts; the excellent linearity of these two properties also holds at other temperatures. An interpretation of this linear

Table 9.3. Solubility $^a$ of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ in molten LiF-BeF $_2$ solvents; enthalpy and entropy of solution   

<table><tr><td>Solvent composition (mole % LiF)</td><td>Temperature range measured (°C)</td><td>Henry&#x27;s law K (mole fraction BF3/atm) at 600°C</td><td>ΔHb(kcal/mole)</td><td>ΔSb(cal mole-1deg-1)</td></tr><tr><td>63c,d</td><td>470–653</td><td>0.00207</td><td>-15.1</td><td>-29.6</td></tr><tr><td>66</td><td>520–725</td><td>0.00242</td><td>-15.6</td><td>-29.9</td></tr><tr><td>70</td><td>548–732</td><td>0.00354</td><td>-15.5</td><td>-29.0</td></tr><tr><td>75</td><td>641–857</td><td>0.00500</td><td>-15.9</td><td>-28.7</td></tr><tr><td>80c</td><td>714–866</td><td>0.00613</td><td>-15.6</td><td>-28.0</td></tr></table>

$a$ Pressure range, 1.3-3.0 atm.   
${}^{b}$ Calculated from the equation $\ln K = \frac{\Delta S}{R} -\frac{\Delta H}{RT}$ .   
${}^{c}$ Measurements in this solvent composition not completed.   
$d$ In the pressure range 1.3-3.0 atm, at temperatures below $520^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , there are positive deviations from Henry's law.

![](images/db45c3530ede9d2c05398258c53a176765c65aac89d4f002d0e0d3854286df2f.jpg)  
Fig. 9.6. Henry's law solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ vs activity of LiF in molten LiF-BeF $_2$ at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

behavior is that the ionic reaction equilibrium

$$
F ^ {-} (1) + B F _ {3} (g) \rightleftharpoons B F _ {4} ^ {-} (1)
$$

occurs, from which it follows that

$$
a _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {4}} = K _ {i} P _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {3}} a _ {\mathrm {F}} -, \tag {1}
$$

![](images/709b59825514ac8925664827889e503599925ff1f4a062b7ecdc27505d3829c9.jpg)  
Fig. 9.7. Solubility of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ in 63-37 mole $\%$ LiF-BeF $_2$ at $517.6 \pm 0.5^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

where $a_{\mathrm{BF}_4^-}$ and $a_{\mathrm{F}}$ are, respectively, the activities of fluoroborate and fluoride ions; $K_{i}$ is the equilibrium constant of the reaction (like any equilibrium constant, $K_{i}$ varies with temperature); and $P_{\mathrm{BF}_3}$ is the equilibrium pressure of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ . At constant temperature and pressure, Eq. (1) explicitly states that $a_{\mathrm{BF}_4^-}$ varies linearly with $a_{\mathrm{F}}$ . In this interpretation of the phenomena depicted in Fig. 9.6, the concentration of dissolved $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is identified with the activity of $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ in the melt, while the activity of LiF is, of course, identified with the activity of fluoride ion in the melt.

In the pressure range under study (1.3-3 atm) the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is sufficiently low that the dissolved molecules do not interact with one another. This noninteraction between solute molecules is the basis of Henry's law. In this investigation virtually all the solubilities of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ obey Henry's law. However, Henry's law does not seem to hold at relatively low temperatures in the melt of composition LiF-BeF $_2$ (63-37 mole

$\%$ ). At temperatures below $520^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the solubilities of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ become 1 mole $\%$ or greater. Figure 9.7 shows data which do not obey Henry's law; although the data exhibit some scatter, the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ per unit pressure almost certainly increases with pressure; that is, the deviations from Henry's law are positive. Further measurement will be made to confirm these deviations.

# 9.9 EQUILIBRIUM PHASE RELATIONSHIPS IN THE SYSTEM RbF-RbBF4

L. O. Gilpatrick C. J. Barton

An investigation of the equilibrium phase diagram for the $\mathrm{RbF - RbBF_4}$ system was completed. Final revisions to the system diagram, as reported previously,[28] resulted from measurements with purer rubidium fluoride than was previously employed.

New RbF has been obtained and purified by treatment with dry $\mathbf{H}_2$ and HF at temperatures up to and above the melting point to remove water and hydroxide. Rubidium fluoride was produced which has a melting point of $793^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which is in good agreement with the literature value of $795^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Binary mixtures of RbF and $\mathrm{RbBF_4}$ were prepared at 5 mole $\%$ intervals ranging up to 20 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{RbBF_4}$ .

28. L. O. Gilpatrick and C. J. Barton, MSR Program Semiannu. Progr. Rep. Feb. 28, 1970, ORNL-4548, p. 133.

Differential thermal analysis (DTA) was used to examine these compositions, which gave the expected higher temperatures in each case, lending support to the belief that the irregularities in the original part of the study were due to RbF of insufficient quality.

The phase diagram in its final form is shown in Fig. 9.8.

# 9.10 ACTIVITIES IN ALKALI FLUORIDE - FLUOROBORATE MIXTURES

D.M.Moulton J.Braunstein

Thermodynamics of mixing in alkali fluoride - alkali fluoroborate mixtures are of interest in connection with proposed coolants for the MSBR. Deviations from ideality, although small, are less well understood in such common cation binary mixtures (and have been less thoroughly studied) than in common anion binary mixtures. Endothermic mixing is frequently observed rather than the exothermic effect expected from electrostatic considerations, and may be associated with changes in packing of large anions of differing size as well as with the dispersion energy of the mixed anions.[29] Here we report some activity coefficient

29. J. L. Holm and O. J. Kleppa, J. Chem. Phys. 49, 2425 (1968); O. J. Kleppa, Annu. Rev. Phys. Chem. 16, 187 (1965); J. Braunstein in Ionic Interactions, S. Petrucci, ed., p. 179, Academic, New York, 1971.

![](images/2a08144df12c6016a13b142e2758843adf03a0af7e0d175ad5a25466887c7f50.jpg)  
Fig. 9.8. The system $\mathbf{RbBF}_4\cdot \mathbf{RbF}$

calculations from available data in fluoride-fluoroborate mixtures.

We have used the phase diagrams of the alkali fluoride-fluoroborate systems (Na, K, Rb) to calculate the component activities along the liquidus lines. The method was the usual one of integrating the Schröder-van Laar equation,

$$
\ln \gamma x = \int_ {T _ {0}} ^ {T} \frac {\Delta H _ {f}}{R T ^ {2}} d T,
$$

to give the ideal liquidus and then comparing actual and ideal mole fractions. A power series was used for the heat capacity difference. Each activity refers to the hypothetical pure liquid at the same temperature. The sources of the data are Barton et al., $^{30}$ Kubaschewski et al., $^{31}$ and Dworkin and Bredig. $^{32}$

In all three cases the behavior of the activity coefficients is qualitatively the same (see Table 9.4). The fluoride shows positive deviation at first but then becomes negative before it reaches the eutectic. The fluoroborates always show negative deviation (with sodium it is only very slight). In both cases the sodium salt shows the smallest and rubidium the largest tendency toward negative deviation.

A comparison with the activity coefficients of Cantor,33 which were derived from decomposition pressure measurements, shows that for NaF we agree fairly well up to about 50 mole $\%$ but then deviate increasingly. For KF the agreement is worse, and for

Table 9.4. Activity coefficients in alkali fluoride-fluoroborate mixtures at $1000^{\circ}\mathbf{K}$ for Na and $1100^{\circ}\mathbf{K}$ for K and Rb   

<table><tr><td>\( X_{BF4} \)</td><td>0.2</td><td>0.4</td><td>0.6</td><td>0.7</td><td>0.8</td><td>0.9</td><td>0.95</td></tr><tr><td>\( γ_{Na} \)</td><td>1.10</td><td>1.20</td><td>1.23</td><td>1.19</td><td>1.12</td><td>0.89</td><td>1.00(-)</td></tr><tr><td>\( γ_{Na}^a \)</td><td>1.15</td><td>1.26</td><td>1.31</td><td>1.32</td><td>1.33</td><td>1.33</td><td>1.00(+)</td></tr><tr><td>\( γ_K \)</td><td>1.05</td><td>1.08</td><td>1.02</td><td>0.97</td><td>0.98</td><td>0.99</td><td>1.00(-)</td></tr><tr><td>\( γ_K^a \)</td><td>1.14</td><td>1.24</td><td>1.32</td><td>1.35</td><td>1.01</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><td>\( γ_{Rb} \)</td><td>1.02</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.93</td><td>0.94</td><td>0.97</td><td>0.99</td><td>0.99</td></tr></table>

$a$ Cantor.

Eutectic: fluoride to left, fluoroborate to right.

the fluoroborates the direction of deviation is the opposite. The activity coefficients are shown; they have been put on the same standard temperature $T_{0}$ as Cantor's by saying that $\gamma_{T_0} = \gamma_T^{(T / T_0)}$ (i.e., ideal entropy of mixing), which does not change them much and in particular does not affect the shape of the curve or the direction of deviation.

The differences in the calculated activity coefficients may indicate a significant temperature dependence of the activity coefficients since the eutectic temperatures lie some 500 to $700^{\circ}$ below the temperatures of the fluoroborate decomposition pressure measurements. Deviations from ideality calculated from the NaF $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and $\mathrm{KF - KBF_4}$ phase diagrams $^{30}$ are compared with those calculated from the NaF-NaI and KF-KI phase diagrams $^{34}$ and are shown as the interaction parameters for excess free energy at the eutectic in Table 9.5.

In both the $\mathrm{BF}_4\text{-F}^-$ and $\mathbf{I}^{-}\mathbf{F}^{-}$ systems, deviations from ideality become less negative with decreasing size of the common cation, as would be expected if changes in anion packing contribute positive deviations.

# 9.11 HIGH-TEMPERATURE CRYSTAL STRUCTURE AND VOLUME OF SODIUM TETRAFLUOROBORATE AND RELATED COMPOUNDS

M. A. Bredig

High-temperature x-ray diffraction patterns of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and $\mathrm{CaSO}_4$ (isostructural) reported by several authors
